Area Calculation of Rhombus (ABCD): - The area of a rhombus can be calculated using the formula ( text{Area} = s^2 sin(theta) ), where (s) is the side length and (theta) is one of the interior angles. - Given (angle BAD = 60^circ) and area 24, we have: [ s^2 sin(60^circ) = 24 implies s^2 cdot frac{sqrt{3}}{2} = 24 implies s^2 = frac{48}{sqrt{3}} = 16sqrt{3} ]2. Diagonals of Rhombus (ABCD): - The diagonals of a rhombus with side length (s) and angle (theta) are given by (d_1 = 2s sin(theta/2)) and (d_2 = 2s cos(theta/2)). - For (theta = 60^circ): [ d_1 = 2s sin(30^circ) = s quad text{and} quad d_2 = 2s cos(30^circ) = ssqrt{3} ] - Thus, the diagonals of (ABCD) are (AC = ssqrt{3}) and (BD = s).3. Similarity and Diagonals of (BFDE): - Since (BFDE) is similar to (ABCD), the ratio of their corresponding diagonals is the same. The diagonal (BD) of (BFDE) corresponds to the diagonal (AC) of (ABCD). - The ratio (k) is: [ k = frac{BD_{text{BFDE}}}{AC_{text{ABCD}}} = frac{s}{ssqrt{3}} = frac{1}{sqrt{3}} ] - The other diagonal (FE) of (BFDE) corresponds to (BD) of (ABCD), giving (FE = frac{1}{sqrt{3}} cdot ssqrt{3} = s).4. Area of Rhombus (BFDE): - The area of a rhombus is (frac{1}{2} times d_1 times d_2). - For (BFDE), the diagonals are (BD = s) and (FE = frac{s}{sqrt{3}}): [ text{Area}_{text{BFDE}} = frac{1}{2} times s times frac{s}{sqrt{3}} = frac{s^2}{2sqrt{3}} ] - Substituting (s^2 = 16sqrt{3}): [ text{Area}_{text{BFDE}} = frac{16sqrt{3}}{2sqrt{3}} = 8 ]Thus, the area of rhombus (BFDE) is (boxed{C}).]

🔑:The ratio of any length on ABCD to a corresponding length on BFDE^2 is equal to the ratio of their areas. Since angle BAD=60, triangle ADB and triangle DBC are equilateral. DB, which is equal to AB, is the diagonal of rhombus ABCD. Therefore, AC=frac{DB(2)}{2sqrt{3}}=frac{DB}{sqrt{3}}. DB and AC are the longer diagonal of rhombuses BEDF and ABCD, respectively. So the ratio of their areas is (frac{1}{sqrt{3}})^2 or frac{1}{3}. One-third the area of ABCD is equal to 8. So the answer is boxed{text{C}}.Solution 2Draw the line overline{DB} to form an equilateral triangle, since angle BAD=60, and line segments overline{AB} and overline{AD} are equal in length. To find the area of the smaller rhombus, we only need to find the value of any arbitrary base, then square the result. To find the value of the base, use the line we just drew and connect it to point E at a right angle along line overline{DB}. Call the connected point P, with triangles DPE and EPB being 30-60-90 triangles, meaning we can find the length of overline{ED} or overline{EB}. The base of ABCD must be sqrt{24}, and half of that length must be sqrt{6}(triangles DPE and EPB are congruent by SSS). Solving for the third length yields sqrt{8}, which we square to get the answer boxed{text{C}}.Solution 3Draw line DB, cutting rhombus BFDE into two triangles which fit nicely into 2/3 of equilateral triangle ABD. Thus the area of BFDE is (2/3)*12=8.

❓:Four, n^{2}(n geqslant 4) positive numbers are arranged in n rows and n columns,begin{array}{llllll}a_{11} & a_{12} & a_{13} & a_{14} & cdots & a_{1 n} a_{21} & a_{22} & a_{23} & a_{24} & cdots & a_{2 n} a_{31} & a_{32} & a_{33} & a_{34} & cdots & a_{3 n} a_{41} & a_{42} & a_{43} & a_{44} & cdots & a_{4 n} cdots & & & & & a_{n 1} & a_{n 2} & a_{n 3} & a_{n 4} & cdots & a_{n n}end{array}where the numbers in each row form an arithmetic sequence, and the numbers in each column form a geometric sequence, and all the common ratios are equal. Given a_{24}=1,a_{42}=frac{1}{8}, a_{43}=frac{3}{16} text {, find } a_{11}+a_{22}+a_{33}+a_{44}+cdots+a_{n n} text {. }

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's see. We have an n x n grid (where n is at least 4) of positive numbers. Each row forms an arithmetic sequence, and each column forms a geometric sequence with all columns having the same common ratio. We're given specific values: a_{24} = 1, a_{42} = 1/8, and a_{43} = 3/16. We need to find the sum of the diagonal elements from a_{11} to a_{nn}.First, let me recall what arithmetic and geometric sequences are. An arithmetic sequence has a common difference between consecutive terms, so each term is the previous term plus a constant. A geometric sequence has a common ratio, so each term is the previous term multiplied by a constant.Since all columns are geometric sequences with the same common ratio, let's denote this common ratio as r. So, for any column j, the element a_{i+1,j} = a_{i,j} * r. That means each element in a column is the element above it multiplied by r.Now, each row is an arithmetic sequence. Let's think about how that interacts with the columns being geometric. Let's consider the first row. Since it's an arithmetic sequence, the difference between consecutive terms is constant. Let's denote the first term as a_{11} and the common difference for the first row as d_1. Then, a_{1k} = a_{11} + (k - 1)d_1 for k from 1 to n.Similarly, for the second row, which is also an arithmetic sequence, let the first term be a_{21} and the common difference be d_2. Then, a_{2k} = a_{21} + (k - 1)d_2.But since the columns are geometric sequences, each element in column j is r times the element above it. So, for example, a_{21} = a_{11} * r, a_{31} = a_{21} * r = a_{11} * r^2, and so on.Similarly, for the second column, a_{12} is part of the first row's arithmetic sequence, so a_{12} = a_{11} + d_1. So each row's common difference is scaled by r^{i-1} where i is the row number.Therefore, the arithmetic sequences in the rows have differences that form a geometric progression themselves, with ratio r.So, for example, the first row has common difference d_1, the second row has d_2 = d_1 * r, the third row has d_3 = d_1 * r^2, etc.This is a key relation.Now, let's try to write expressions for the given elements: a_{24}, a_{42}, and a_{43}.First, a_{24} is in row 2, column 4.Since row 2 is an arithmetic sequence with first term a_{21} and common difference d_2, then:a_{24} = a_{21} + (4 - 1)d_2 = a_{21} + 3d_2But a_{21} is also equal to a_{11} * r, since column 1 is a geometric sequence.Similarly, d_2 = d_1 * r (from earlier relation).Therefore, substituting:a_{24} = a_{11} * r + 3d_1 * rWe know a_{24} = 1, so:1 = r(a_{11} + 3d_1) --- (1)Next, a_{42} is in row 4, column 2.Column 2 is a geometric sequence, so a_{42} = a_{12} * r^{3} (since it's the 4th row, so exponent 3).But a_{12} is the second term in the first row's arithmetic sequence: a_{12} = a_{11} + d_1.Therefore:a_{42} = (a_{11} + d_1) * r^3Given that a_{42} = 1/8, so:1/8 = (a_{11} + d_1) * r^3 --- (2)Similarly, a_{43} is in row 4, column 3.Column 3 is a geometric sequence, so a_{43} = a_{13} * r^{3}But a_{13} is the third term in the first row's arithmetic sequence: a_{13} = a_{11} + 2d_1Thus:a_{43} = (a_{11} + 2d_1) * r^3Given that a_{43} = 3/16, so:3/16 = (a_{11} + 2d_1) * r^3 --- (3)Now, we have three equations: (1), (2), and (3). An arithmetic sequence has a common difference between consecutive terms, so in a row, each term is the previous term plus some constant. A geometric sequence has a common ratio between consecutive terms, so in a column, each term is the previous term multiplied by a common ratio. Also, all columns share the same common ratio, which is important.Given that the columns are geometric sequences with the same ratio, let's denote this common ratio as r. So, moving down any column, each entry is r times the one above it. That means, for example, a_{i+1,j} = r * a_{i,j} for any i and j.Now, the rows are arithmetic sequences. That means, in any row i, the difference between consecutive entries is constant. Let's denote the common difference for row i as d_i. The formula for the angle bisector between two vectors AB and AD is given by normalized vectors. AB vector is (3,0), so normalized is (1,0). AD vector is (a,b), normalized is (a/sqrt(a² + b²), b/sqrt(a² + b²)). The angle bisector direction is the sum of these two unit vectors: (1 + a/sqrt(a² + b²), 0 + b/sqrt(a² + b²)). 🔑:5. D.As shown in Figure 6, extend B E to intersect A D at point H. It is easy to see thatbegin{array}{c}triangle A H E cong triangle A B E Rightarrow E H=B E, A H=A B=3 .end{array}Then H D=A D-A H=7-3=4 text {. }Thus, E F is the midline of trapezoid H B C D.Therefore, E G and G F are the midlines of triangle B H D and triangle B C D, respectively.Hence E G: G F=H D: B C=4: 7.

❓:19. Let x, y, z, w be different positive real numbers such that x+frac{1}{y}=y+frac{1}{z}=z+frac{1}{w}=w+frac{1}{x}=t. Find t.(2 marks)Let x, y, z, w be different positive real numbers such that x+frac{1}{y}=y+frac{1}{z}=z+frac{1}{w}=w+frac{1}{x}=t. Find t.(2 marks)

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I have this problem where there are four different positive real numbers: x, y, z, w. They satisfy the equations x + 1/y = y + 1/z = z + 1/w = w + 1/x = t. I need to find the value of t. Hmm, let's see how to approach this.First, since all those expressions equal t, maybe I can set up equations by equating them to each other. Let me write down each equation:1. x + 1/y = t2. y + 1/z = t3. z + 1/w = t4. w + 1/x = tSo, there are four equations here with four variables. The challenge is that they are all cyclic and interconnected. Since the variables are different positive real numbers, I can't assume symmetry where all variables are equal. But maybe there's a pattern or a substitution that can help me solve this.Let me try to express each variable in terms of the next. Since f is a Möbius transformation, composing it multiple times can be represented by matrix multiplication. The transformation f(v) = (t v - 1)/v corresponds to the matrix:M = [ t -1 ] [ 1 0 ]Then, the composition of f four times corresponds to M^4. To have f^4(v) = v, the matrix M^4 must be a scalar multiple of the identity matrix. That is, M^4 = k I for some scalar k. Then, the transformation would satisfy f^4(v) = (k v + ... ) / (... ) = v. If M^4 is scalar, then the transformation is equivalent to the identity.So, to find t such that M^4 is a scalar matrix. Let's compute M^4.First, compute M^2:M^2 = M * M = [ t -1 ] [ t -1 ] = [ t*t + (-1)*1 , t*(-1) + (-1)*0 ] [ 1 0 ] [ 1 0 ] [ 1*t + 0*1 , 1*(-1) + 0*0 ]= [ t^2 - 1 , -t ] [ t , -1 ]Then, M^3 = M^2 * M:= [ t^2 - 1 , -t ] [ t -1 ] [ t , -1 ] [ 1 0 ]First row:(t^2 - 1)*t + (-t)*1 = t^3 - t - t = t^3 - 2t(t^2 - 1)*(-1) + (-t)*0 = -t^2 + 1Second row:t*t + (-1)*1 = t^2 - 1t*(-1) + (-1)*0 = -tSo, M^3 = [ t^3 - 2t , -t^2 + 1 ] [ t^2 - 1 , -t ]Then, M^4 = M^3 * M:= [ t^3 - 2t , -t^2 + 1 ] [ t -1 ] [ t^2 - 1 , -t ] [ 1 0 ]First row:(t^3 - 2t)*t + (-t^2 + 1)*1 = t^4 - 2t^2 - t^2 + 1 = t^4 - 3t^2 + 1(t^3 - 2t)*(-1) + (-t^2 + 1)*0 = -t^3 + 2tSecond row:(t^2 - 1)*t + (-t)*1 = t^3 - t - t = t^3 - 2t(t^2 - 1)*(-1) + (-t)*0 = -t^2 + 1So, M^4 = [ t^4 - 3t^2 + 1 , -t^3 + 2t ] [ t^3 - 2t , -t^2 + 1 ]For M^4 to be a scalar multiple of the identity matrix, the off-diagonal elements must be zero, and the diagonal elements must be equal.So, set:-t^3 + 2t = 0 --> t(-t^2 + 2) = 0. Since t is positive, t ≠ 0, so -t^2 + 2 = 0 --> t^2 = 2 --> t = √2Similarly, the lower-left element: t^3 - 2t = t(t^2 - 2). If t = √2, then t^2 - 2 = 0, so this term is zero.Then, diagonal elements:Top-left: t^4 - 3t^2 + 1With t = √2, t^4 = (√2)^4 = 4, t^2 = 2So, 4 - 3*2 + 1 = 4 - 6 + 1 = -1Bottom-right: -t^2 + 1 = -2 + 1 = -1So, both diagonal elements are -1, and off-diagonal are zero. So, M^4 = -I. Therefore, M^4 is indeed a scalar multiple of the identity matrix (-1 * I). 🔑:19. sqrt{2}19. Expressing w, z and y in terms of t and x, we havew=frac{x t-1}{x}, quad z=frac{x t^{2}-t-x}{x t-1} text{ and } quad y=frac{x t^{3}-t^{2}-2 x t+1}{x t^{2}-t-x}.Substituting the last expression into x+frac{1}{y}=t, we havebegin{aligned}x+frac{x t^{2}-t-x}{x t^{3}-t^{2}-2 x t+1} & =t x t^{4}-x^{2} t^{3}-t^{3}-2 x t^{2}+2 x^{2} t+2 t & =0 tleft(t^{2}-2right)left(x t-x^{2}-1right) & =0end{aligned}Note that the last factor on the left hand side of the last row is non-zero, for otherwise we get x+frac{1}{x}=t=x+frac{1}{y} which contradicts the fact that x neq y. Since t>0, we must have t=sqrt{2}.Remark. Owing to some typo, sqrt{2},-sqrt{2} and pm sqrt{2} were all accepted as correct answers in the live paper.
